<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Ludwig van Beethoven</h1>

<p><b>Ludwig van Beethoven</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'oo' in 'foot'">ʊ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-LudwigVanBeethoven.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/En-LudwigVanBeethoven.ogg" title="En-LudwigVanBeethoven.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span>, <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>German:</small> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈluːtvɪç fan ˈbeːtˌhoˑfn̩]</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Ludwig_van_Beethoven.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/De-Ludwig_van_Beethoven.ogg" title="De-Ludwig van Beethoven.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>; baptised 17 December 1770<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_date.3F_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-birth_date.3F-1">[1]</a></sup> – 26 March 1827) was a German <a href="/wiki/Composer" title="Composer">composer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pianist" title="Pianist">pianist</a>. A crucial figure in the transition between the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic</a> eras in <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">Western art music</a>, he remains one of the most famous and influential of all composers. His best-known compositions include 9 <a href="/wiki/Symphony" title="Symphony">symphonies</a>, 5 <a href="/wiki/Piano_concerto" title="Piano concerto">piano concertos</a>, 1 <a href="/wiki/Violin_concerto" title="Violin concerto">violin concerto</a>, 32 <a href="/wiki/Piano_sonata" title="Piano sonata">piano sonatas</a>, 16 <a href="/wiki/String_quartet" title="String quartet">string quartets</a>, his great <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">Mass</a> the <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i>, and one <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>.</p>
<p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, then the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Cologne" title="Electorate of Cologne">Electorate of Cologne</a> and part of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, Beethoven displayed his musical talents at an early age and was taught by his father <a href="/wiki/Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Johann van Beethoven">Johann van Beethoven</a> and by composer and conductor <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe" title="Christian Gottlob Neefe">Christian Gottlob Neefe</a>. At the age of 21 he moved to <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, where he began studying composition with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> and gained a reputation as a virtuoso pianist. He lived in Vienna until his death. By his late 20s his hearing began to deteriorate, and by the last decade of his life he was almost completely deaf. In 1811 he gave up conducting and performing in public but continued to compose; many of his most admired works come from these last 15 years of his life.</p>
<p></p>

</div>
</div>
<p>Beethoven was the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven_(1712%E2%80%931773)" title="Ludwig van Beethoven (1712–1773)">Ludwig van Beethoven</a> (1712–73), a musician from the town of <a href="/wiki/Mechelen" title="Mechelen">Mechelen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brabant" title="Duchy of Brabant">Duchy of Brabant</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flemish_region" title="Flemish region">Flemish region</a> of what is now <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, who at the age of twenty moved to Bonn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroveOnline1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-GroveOnline1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008407_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008407-3">[3]</a></sup> Ludwig (he adopted the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i><span lang="nl">Lodewijk</span></i>) was employed as a <a href="/wiki/Bass_(voice_type)" title="Bass (voice type)">bass</a> singer at the court of the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Cologne" title="Electorate of Cologne">Elector of Cologne</a>, eventually rising to become, in 1761, <span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a></span> (music director) and thereafter the pre-eminent musician in Bonn. The portrait he commissioned of himself towards the end of his life remained proudly displayed in his grandson's rooms as a talisman of his musical heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford201512.E2.80.9317_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford201512.E2.80.9317-4">[4]</a></sup> Ludwig had one son, <a href="/wiki/Johann_van_Beethoven" title="Johann van Beethoven">Johann</a> (1740–1792), who worked as a tenor in the same musical establishment and gave keyboard and <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> lessons to supplement his income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroveOnline1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-GroveOnline1-2">[2]</a></sup> Johann married <a href="/wiki/Maria_Magdalena_Keverich" title="Maria Magdalena Keverich">Maria Magdalena Keverich</a> in 1767; she was the daughter of Johann Heinrich Keverich (1701–1751), who had been the head chef at the court of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_Trier" title="Archbishopric of Trier">Archbishopric of Trier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192149_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192149-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="3336" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg/220px-Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg/330px-Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg/440px-Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universit%C3%A4t_Bonn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Electoral_Palace,_Bonn" title="Electoral Palace, Bonn">Prince-Elector's Palace</a> (<i><span lang="de">Kurfürstliches Schloss</span></i>) in Bonn, where the Beethoven family had been active since the 1730s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beethoven was born of this marriage in Bonn. There is no authentic record of the date of his birth; however, the registry of his baptism, in a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> service at the Parish of St. Regius on 17 December 1770, survives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> As children of that era were traditionally baptised the day after birth in the Catholic Rhine country, and it is known that Beethoven's family and his teacher <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Albrechtsberger" title="Johann Georg Albrechtsberger">Johann Albrechtsberger</a> celebrated his birthday on 16 December, most scholars accept 16 December 1770 as Beethoven's date of birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192153_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192153-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon2000chapter_1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon2000chapter_1-8">[8]</a></sup> Of the seven children born to Johann van Beethoven, only Ludwig, the second-born, and two younger brothers survived infancy. <a href="/wiki/Kaspar_Anton_Karl_van_Beethoven" title="Kaspar Anton Karl van Beethoven">Kaspar Anton Karl</a> was born on 8 April 1774, and Nikolaus Johann, the youngest, was born on 2 October 1776.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanley7_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanley7-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beethoven's first music teacher was his father. He later had other local teachers: the court <a href="/wiki/Organist" title="Organist">organist</a> Gilles van den Eeden (d. 1782), Tobias Friedrich Pfeiffer (a family friend, who provided keyboard tuition), and Franz Rovantini (a relative, who instructed him in playing the violin and <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroveOnline1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-GroveOnline1-2">[2]</a></sup> From the outset his tuition regime, which began in his fifth year, was harsh and intensive, often reducing him to tears; with the involvement of the insomniac Pfeiffer there were irregular late-night sessions with the young Beethoven being dragged from his bed to the keyboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford201522.2C_32_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford201522.2C_32-10">[10]</a></sup> Beethoven's musical talent was obvious at a young age. Johann, aware of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Mozart" title="Leopold Mozart">Leopold Mozart</a>'s successes in this area (with his son <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang</a> and daughter <a href="/wiki/Maria_Anna_Mozart" title="Maria Anna Mozart">Nannerl</a>), attempted to promote his son as a <a href="/wiki/Child_prodigy" title="Child prodigy">child prodigy</a>, claiming that Beethoven was six (he was seven) on the posters for Beethoven's first public performance in March 1778.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192159_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192159-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some time after 1779, Beethoven began his studies with his most important teacher in Bonn, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Neefe" title="Christian Gottlob Neefe">Christian Gottlob Neefe</a>, who was appointed the Court's Organist in that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192167_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192167-12">[12]</a></sup> Neefe taught Beethoven composition, and by March 1783 had helped him write his first published composition: a set of keyboard variations (<a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a> 63).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanley7_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stanley7-9">[9]</a></sup> Beethoven soon began working with Neefe as assistant organist, at first unpaid (1781), and then as a paid employee (1784) of the court chapel conducted by the Kapellmeister <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Luchesi" title="Andrea Luchesi">Andrea Luchesi</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Three_Piano_Sonatas,_WoO_47_(Beethoven)" title="Three Piano Sonatas, WoO 47 (Beethoven)">first three piano sonatas</a>, named "<span lang="de"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurf%C3%BCrst" title="Kurfürst">Kurfürst</a></span>" ("Elector") for their dedication to the Elector <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_Friedrich_von_K%C3%B6nigsegg-Rothenfels" title="Maximilian Friedrich von Königsegg-Rothenfels">Maximilian Friedrich</a> (1708–1784), were published in 1783. Maximilian Frederick noticed Beethoven's talent early, and subsidised and encouraged the young man's musical studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer192171.E2.80.9374_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer192171.E2.80.9374-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1132" data-file-width="912" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg/170px-Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg/255px-Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg/340px-Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thirteen-year-old_Beethoven.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A portrait of the 13-year-old Beethoven by an unknown Bonn master (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1783</span>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Maximilian Frederick's successor as the Elector of Bonn was <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Maximilian_Francis_of_Austria" title="Archduke Maximilian Francis of Austria">Maximilian Francis</a>, the youngest son of Empress <a href="/wiki/Maria_Theresa" title="Maria Theresa">Maria Theresa</a> of Austria, and he brought notable changes to Bonn. Echoing changes made in Vienna by his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_II_of_Austria" title="Joseph II of Austria">Joseph</a>, he introduced reforms based on <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment philosophy</a>, with increased support for education and the arts. The teenage Beethoven was almost certainly influenced by these changes. He may also have been influenced at this time by ideas prominent in <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">freemasonry</a>, as Neefe and others around Beethoven were members of the local chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Illuminati" title="Illuminati">Order of the Illuminati</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200815_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200815-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1787 Beethoven travelled to Vienna, possibly at another's expense, for the first time, apparently in the hope of studying with Mozart. The details of <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_and_Mozart" title="Beethoven and Mozart">their relationship</a> are uncertain, including whether they actually met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200823_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200823-15">[15]</a></sup> Having learned that his mother was ill, Beethoven returned about two weeks after his arrival in Vienna. His mother died shortly thereafter, and his father lapsed deeper into <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>. As a result, Beethoven became responsible for the care of his two younger brothers, and spent the next five years in Bonn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200824_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200824-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beethoven was introduced in these years to several people who became important in his life. Franz Wegeler, a young medical student, introduced him to the von Breuning family (one of whose daughters Wegeler eventually married). Beethoven often visited the von Breuning household, where he taught piano to some of the children. Here he encountered German and classical literature. The von Breuning family environment was less stressful than his own, which was increasingly dominated by his father's decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200816_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200816-17">[17]</a></sup> Beethoven also came to the attention of <a href="/wiki/Count_Ferdinand_Ernst_Gabriel_von_Waldstein" title="Count Ferdinand Ernst Gabriel von Waldstein">Count Ferdinand von Waldstein</a>, who became a lifelong friend and financial supporter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921102_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921102-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1789 Beethoven obtained a legal order by which half of his father's salary was paid directly to him for support of the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921104_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921104-19">[19]</a></sup> He also contributed further to the family's income by playing <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a> in the court orchestra. This familiarised Beethoven with a variety of operas, including three by Mozart that were performed at court in this period. He also befriended <a href="/wiki/Anton_Reicha" title="Anton Reicha">Anton Reicha</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flautist</a> and violinist of about his own age who was a nephew of the court orchestra's conductor, <a href="/wiki/Josef_Reicha" title="Josef Reicha">Josef Reicha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921105.E2.80.9309_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921105.E2.80.9309-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1790 to 1792, Beethoven composed a significant number of works (none were published at the time, and most are now listed as <a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">works without opus number</a>) that demonstrated his growing range and maturity. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musicologist" title="Musicologist">Musicologists</a> have identified a theme similar to those of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Symphony</a> in a set of variations written in 1791.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200835.E2.80.9341_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200835.E2.80.9341-21">[21]</a></sup> It was probably on Neefe's recommendation that Beethoven received his first commissions. The municipal leaders in Bonn had commissioned cantatas to mark the occasion of the death in 1790 of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor">Franz Joseph II</a> and the subsequent accession of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Leopold II</a> as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>. The two <i>Emperor Cantatas</i> (WoO 87, WoO 88) Beethoven scored were never performed at the time and they remained lost until the 1880s. But they were, according to <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Brahms</a>, distinctively "Beethoven through and through" and as such prophetic of the tragic style which would mark his music as distinct from the classical tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beethoven was probably first introduced to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> in late 1790, when the latter was travelling to London and stopped in Bonn around Christmas time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200835_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200835-23">[23]</a></sup> A year and a half later, they met in Bonn on Haydn's return trip from London to Vienna in July 1792, and it is likely that arrangements were made at that time for Beethoven to study with the old master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200841_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200841-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishing_his_career_in_Vienna">Establishing his career in Vienna</span></h3>
<p>With the Elector's help, Beethoven left Bonn for Vienna in November 1792, amid rumours of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Coalition" title="First Coalition">war spilling out of France</a>; he learned shortly after his arrival that his father had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921124_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921124-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921148_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921148-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200842_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200842-27">[27]</a></sup> Mozart had also recently died. Count Waldstein, in his farewell note to Beethoven, wrote: "Through uninterrupted diligence you will receive Mozart's spirit through Haydn's hands."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200842_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200842-27">[27]</a></sup> Over the next few years, Beethoven responded to the widespread feeling that he was a successor to the recently deceased Mozart by studying that master's work and writing works with a distinctly Mozartean flavour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200843_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200843-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="425" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Riedel_1801.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of Beethoven as a young man by Carl Traugott Riedel (1769–1832)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beethoven did not immediately set out to establish himself as a composer, but rather devoted himself to study and performance. Working under Haydn's direction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroveOnline3_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-GroveOnline3-29">[29]</a></sup> he sought to master <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">counterpoint</a>. He also studied violin under <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Schuppanzigh" title="Ignaz Schuppanzigh">Ignaz Schuppanzigh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200847.2C_54_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200847.2C_54-30">[30]</a></sup> Early in this period, he also began receiving occasional instruction from <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Salieri" title="Antonio Salieri">Antonio Salieri</a>, primarily in Italian vocal composition style; this relationship persisted until at least 1802, and possibly 1809.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921161_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921161-31">[31]</a></sup> With Haydn's departure for England in 1794, Beethoven was expected by the Elector to return home. He chose instead to remain in Vienna, continuing his instruction in counterpoint with <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Albrechtsberger" title="Johann Georg Albrechtsberger">Johann Albrechtsberger</a> and other teachers. Although his stipend from the Elector expired, a number of Viennese noblemen had already recognised his ability and offered him financial support, among them <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Franz_von_Lobkowitz" title="Joseph Franz von Lobkowitz">Prince Joseph Franz Lobkowitz</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl_Alois_Johann-Nepomuk_Vinzenz,_F%C3%BCrst_Lichnowsky" title="Karl Alois Johann-Nepomuk Vinzenz, Fürst Lichnowsky">Prince Karl Lichnowsky</a>, and Baron <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_van_Swieten" title="Gottfried van Swieten">Gottfried van Swieten</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200853_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200853-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1793, Beethoven had established a reputation as an improviser in the salons of the nobility, often playing the <a href="/wiki/Prelude_(music)" title="Prelude (music)">preludes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugues</a> of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">J. S. Bach</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Well-Tempered_Clavier" title="Well-Tempered Clavier">Well-Tempered Clavier</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cross59_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cross59-33">[33]</a></sup> His friend <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Simrock" title="Nikolaus Simrock">Nikolaus Simrock</a> had begun publishing his compositions; the first are believed to be a set of variations (WoO 66).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200846_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200846-34">[34]</a></sup> By 1793, he had established a reputation in Vienna as a piano virtuoso, but he apparently withheld works from publication so that their publication in 1795 would have greater impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200853_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200853-32">[32]</a></sup> Beethoven's first public performance in Vienna was in March 1795, a concert in which he first performed one of his <a href="/wiki/Piano_concerto" title="Piano concerto">piano concertos</a>. It is uncertain whether this was the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 1 (Beethoven)">First</a> or <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second</a>. Documentary evidence is unclear, and both concertos were in a similar state of near-completion (neither was completed or published for several years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200859_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200859-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005144_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005144-36">[36]</a></sup> Shortly after this performance, he arranged for the publication of the first of his compositions to which he assigned an <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">opus number</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Trios,_Op._1_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trios, Op. 1 (Beethoven)">three piano trios, Opus 1</a>. These works were dedicated to his patron Prince Lichnowsky,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200859_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200859-35">[35]</a></sup> and were a financial success; Beethoven's profits were nearly sufficient to cover his living expenses for a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200856_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200856-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_maturity">Musical maturity</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven composed his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._1%E2%80%936,_Op._18_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 1–6, Op. 18 (Beethoven)">first six string quartets (Op. 18)</a> between 1798 and 1800 (commissioned by, and dedicated to, Prince Lobkowitz). They were published in 1801. With premieres of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Beethoven)">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second</a> Symphonies in 1800 and 1803, Beethoven became regarded as one of the most important of a generation of young composers following Haydn and Mozart. He also continued to write in other forms, turning out widely known <a href="/wiki/Piano_sonata" title="Piano sonata">piano sonatas</a> like the "<a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 8 (Beethoven)">Pathétique</a>" sonata (Op. 13), which Cooper describes as "surpass[ing] any of his previous compositions, in strength of character, depth of emotion, level of originality, and ingenuity of motivic and tonal manipulation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200882_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200882-38">[38]</a></sup> He also completed his <a href="/wiki/Septet_(Beethoven)" title="Septet (Beethoven)">Septet</a> (Op. 20) in 1799, which was one of his most popular works during his lifetime.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="912" data-file-width="800" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Hornemann.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Beethoven in 1803, painted by <a href="/wiki/Christian_Horneman" title="Christian Horneman">Christian Horneman</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For the premiere of his <i>First Symphony</i>, Beethoven hired the <a href="/wiki/Burgtheater" title="Burgtheater">Burgtheater</a> on 2 April 1800, and staged an extensive programme of music, including works by Haydn and Mozart, as well as his Septet, the First Symphony, and one of his piano concertos (the latter three works all then unpublished). The concert, which the <i><a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_musikalische_Zeitung" title="Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung">Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung</a></i> described as "the most interesting concert in a long time," was not without difficulties; among the criticisms was that "the players did not bother to pay any attention to the soloist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200890_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200890-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mozart and Haydn were undeniable influences. For example, Beethoven's <a href="/wiki/Quintet_for_Piano_and_Winds_(Beethoven)" title="Quintet for Piano and Winds (Beethoven)">quintet for piano and winds</a> is said to bear a strong resemblance to <a href="/wiki/Quintet_for_Piano_and_Winds_(Mozart)" title="Quintet for Piano and Winds (Mozart)">Mozart's work for the same configuration</a>, albeit with his own distinctive touches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200866_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200866-40">[40]</a></sup> But Beethoven's melodies, musical development, use of modulation and texture, and characterisation of emotion all set him apart from his influences, and heightened the impact some of his early works made when they were first published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200858_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200858-41">[41]</a></sup> By the end of 1800, Beethoven and his music were already much in demand from patrons and publishers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200897_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200897-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1515" data-file-width="1276" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Beethoven_3.jpg/170px-Beethoven_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Beethoven_3.jpg/255px-Beethoven_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Beethoven_3.jpg/340px-Beethoven_3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ludwig van Beethoven: detail of an <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_(M%C3%A4hler,_1804%E2%80%9305)" title="Beethoven (Mähler, 1804–05)">1804–05 portrait</a> by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Willibrord_M%C3%A4hler" title="Joseph Willibrord Mähler">Joseph Willibrord Mähler</a>. The <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/BeethovenWithLyreGuitar%28_W._J._Mahler_-_1804%29.jpg" title="BeethovenWithLyreGuitar( W. J. Mahler - 1804).jpg">complete painting</a> depicts Beethoven with a <a href="/wiki/Lyre-guitar" title="Lyre-guitar">lyre-guitar</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In May 1799, Beethoven taught piano to the daughters of Hungarian Countess Anna Brunsvik. During this time, Beethoven fell in love with the younger daughter <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Brunsvik" title="Josephine Brunsvik">Josephine</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> who has therefore been identified as one of the more likely candidates for the addressee of his letter to the "<a href="/wiki/Immortal_Beloved" title="Immortal Beloved">Immortal Beloved</a>" (in 1812). Shortly after these lessons, Josephine was married to Count Josef Deym. Beethoven was a regular visitor at their house, continuing to teach Josephine, and playing at parties and concerts. Her marriage was by all accounts happy (despite initial financial problems),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and the couple had four children. Her relationship with Beethoven intensified after Deym died suddenly in 1804.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200880_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200880-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beethoven had few other students. From 1801 to 1805, he tutored <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Ries" title="Ferdinand Ries">Ferdinand Ries</a>, who went on to become a composer and later wrote <i>Beethoven remembered</i>, a book about their encounters. The young <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a> studied with Beethoven from 1801 to 1803. Czerny went on to become a renowned music teacher himself, instructing <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>, and gave on 11 February 1812 the Vienna premiere of Beethoven's <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 5 (Beethoven)">fifth piano concerto (the "Emperor")</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1921526_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1921526-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beethoven's compositions between 1800 and 1802 were dominated by two large-scale orchestral works, although he continued to produce other important works such as the piano sonata <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 14 (Beethoven)">Sonata quasi una fantasia</a> known as the "Moonlight Sonata". In the spring of 1801 he completed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Creatures_of_Prometheus" title="The Creatures of Prometheus">The Creatures of Prometheus</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Ballet" title="Ballet">ballet</a>. The work received numerous performances in 1801 and 1802, and Beethoven rushed to publish a piano arrangement to capitalise on its early popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200898.E2.80.93103_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200898.E2.80.93103-47">[47]</a></sup> In the spring of 1802 he completed the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._2_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 2 (Beethoven)">Second Symphony</a>, intended for performance at a concert that was cancelled. The symphony received its premiere instead at a subscription concert in April 1803 at the <a href="/wiki/Theater_an_der_Wien" title="Theater an der Wien">Theater an der Wien</a>, where Beethoven had been appointed composer in residence. In addition to the Second Symphony, the concert also featured the First Symphony, the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Piano Concerto</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Christ_on_the_Mount_of_Olives_(Beethoven)" title="Christ on the Mount of Olives (Beethoven)">Christ on the Mount of Olives</a></i>. Reviews were mixed, but the concert was a financial success; Beethoven was able to charge three times the cost of a typical concert ticket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008112.E2.80.9327_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008112.E2.80.9327-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beethoven's business dealings with publishers also began to improve in 1802 when his brother Kaspar, who had previously assisted him casually, began to assume a larger role in the management of his affairs. In addition to negotiating higher prices for recently composed works, Kaspar also began selling some of Beethoven's earlier unpublished works, and encouraged Beethoven (against the latter's preference) to also make arrangements and transcriptions of his more popular works for other instrument combinations. Beethoven acceded to these requests, as he could not prevent publishers from hiring others to do similar arrangements of his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008112.E2.80.9315_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008112.E2.80.9315-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_hearing">Loss of hearing</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven is reported to have dated his hearing loss from a fit he suffered in 1798 induced by a rage at the interruption of his work—having fallen over, he got up to find himself deaf. His hearing only ever partially recovered and, during its gradual decline, was impeded by a severe form of <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">tinnitus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwafford2015223.E2.80.9324_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwafford2015223.E2.80.9324-50">[50]</a></sup> As early as 1801, he wrote to friends describing his symptoms and the difficulties they caused in both professional and social settings (although it is likely some of his close friends were already aware of the problems).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008108_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008108-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cause of Beethoven's deafness is unknown, but has variously been attributed to <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auto-immune_disorder" title="Auto-immune disorder">auto-immune disorders</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Systemic_lupus_erythematosus" title="Systemic lupus erythematosus">systemic lupus erythematosus</a>), and even his habit of immersing his head in cold water to stay awake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The explanation from Beethoven's autopsy was that he had a "distended inner ear," which developed lesions over time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="369" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/170px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/255px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg/340px-Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_M%C3%A4hler_1815.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Beethoven in 1815 portrait by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Willibrord_M%C3%A4hler" title="Joseph Willibrord Mähler">Joseph Willibrord Mähler</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beethoven, on the advice of his doctor, lived in the small Austrian town of <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt,_Vienna" title="Heiligenstadt, Vienna">Heiligenstadt</a>, just outside Vienna, from April to October 1802 in an attempt to come to terms with his condition. There he wrote his <a href="/wiki/Heiligenstadt_Testament" title="Heiligenstadt Testament">Heiligenstadt Testament</a>, a letter to his brothers which records his thoughts of suicide due to his growing deafness and records his resolution to continue living for and through his art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008120_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008120-53">[53]</a></sup> Over time, his hearing loss became profound: at the end of the premiere of his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a> in 1824, he had to be turned around to see the tumultuous applause of the audience because he could hear neither it nor the orchestra. Beethoven's hearing loss did not prevent him from composing music, but it made playing at concerts—a lucrative source of income—increasingly difficult. After a failed attempt in 1811 to perform his own <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 5 (Beethoven)">Piano Concerto No. 5 (the "Emperor")</a>, which was premiered by his student <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a>, he never performed in public again until he directed the premiere performance of the Ninth Symphony in 1824, which involved him giving cues to conductor Michael Umlauf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>A large collection of Beethoven's hearing aids, such as a special <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ear_horn" title="Ear horn">ear horn</a>, can be viewed at the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_House" title="Beethoven House">Beethoven House</a> Museum in Bonn, Germany. Despite his obvious distress, Czerny remarked that Beethoven could still hear speech and music normally until 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Around 1814 however, by the age of 44, Beethoven was almost totally deaf, and when a group of visitors saw him play a loud arpeggio of thundering bass notes at his piano remarking, "Ist es nicht schön?" (Is it not beautiful?), they felt deep sympathy considering his courage and sense of humour (he lost the ability to hear higher frequencies first).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon2000_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon2000-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of Beethoven's hearing loss, his conversation books are an unusually rich written resource. Used primarily in the last ten or so years of his life, his friends wrote in these books so that he could know what they were saying, and he then responded either orally or in the book. The books contain discussions about music and other matters, and give insights into Beethoven's thinking; they are a source for investigations into how he intended his music should be performed, and also his perception of his relationship to art. Out of a total of 400 conversation books, it has been suggested that 264 were destroyed (and others were altered) after Beethoven's death by his secretary <a href="/wiki/Anton_Schindler" title="Anton Schindler">Anton Schindler</a>, who wished only an idealised biography of the composer to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clive239_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clive239-57">[57]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Albrecht" title="Theodore Albrecht">Theodore Albrecht</a> contests the verity of Schindler's destruction of a large number of conversation books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patronage">Patronage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1829" data-file-width="1378" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/170px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/255px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg/340px-Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rudolf-habsburg-olmuetz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Beethoven's patron, <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Rudolf_of_Austria_(1788%E2%80%931831)" title="Archduke Rudolf of Austria (1788–1831)">Archduke Rudolph</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While Beethoven earned income from publication of his works and from public performances, he also depended on the generosity of patrons for income, for whom he gave private performances and copies of works they commissioned for an exclusive period prior to their publication. Some of his early patrons, including Prince Lobkowitz and Prince Lichnowsky, gave him annual stipends in addition to commissioning works and purchasing published works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper200878.E2.80.9379_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper200878.E2.80.9379-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Perhaps Beethoven's most important aristocratic patron was <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Rudolf_of_Austria_(1788%E2%80%931831)" title="Archduke Rudolf of Austria (1788–1831)">Archduke Rudolph</a>, the youngest son of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Leopold II</a>, who in 1803 or 1804 began to study piano and composition with Beethoven. The cleric (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal-Priest" title="Cardinal-Priest">Cardinal-Priest</a>) and the composer became friends, and their meetings continued until 1824.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005300.E2.80.9301_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005300.E2.80.9301-60">[60]</a></sup> Beethoven dedicated 14 compositions to Rudolph, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piano_Trio_No._7_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Trio No. 7 (Beethoven)">Archduke Trio</a> (1811) and <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa solemnis</a></i> (1823). Rudolph, in turn, dedicated one of his own compositions to Beethoven. The letters Beethoven wrote to Rudolph are today kept at the <a href="/wiki/Gesellschaft_der_Musikfreunde" title="Gesellschaft der Musikfreunde">Gesellschaft der Musikfreunde</a> in Vienna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Another patron was Count (later Prince) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrey_Kirillovich_Razumovsky" title="Andrey Kirillovich Razumovsky">Andreas Razumovsky</a>, for whom the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._7%E2%80%939,_Op._59_%E2%80%93_Rasumovsky_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 7–9, Op. 59 – Rasumovsky (Beethoven)">String Quartets Nos. 7–9, Op. 59, <i>Rasumovsky</i></a> were named.</p>
<p>In the autumn of 1808, after having been rejected for a position at the royal theatre, Beethoven received an offer from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a>'s brother <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Bonaparte" title="Jérôme Bonaparte">Jérôme Bonaparte</a>, then king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Westphalia" title="Kingdom of Westphalia">Westphalia</a>, for a well-paid position as <a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a> at the court in <a href="/wiki/Kassel" title="Kassel">Cassel</a>. To persuade him to stay in Vienna, the Archduke Rudolph, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand,_5th_Prince_Kinsky_of_Wchinitz_and_Tettau" title="Ferdinand, 5th Prince Kinsky of Wchinitz and Tettau">Prince Kinsky</a> and Prince Lobkowitz, after receiving representations from the composer's friends, pledged to pay Beethoven a pension of 4000 florins a year. Only Archduke Rudolph paid his share of the pension on the agreed date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008195_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008195-62">[62]</a></sup> Kinsky, immediately called to military duty, did not contribute and soon died after falling from his horse. Lobkowitz stopped paying in September 1811. No successors came forward to continue the patronage, and Beethoven relied mostly on selling composition rights and a small pension after 1815. The effects of these financial arrangements were undermined to some extent by <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">war with France</a>, which caused significant inflation when the government printed money to fund its war efforts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_period">Middle period</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3796" data-file-width="1790" height="361" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg/170px-Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg/255px-Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg/340px-Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_monument_bonn_muensterplatz_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Beethoven_Monument,_Bonn" title="Beethoven Monument, Bonn">Beethoven Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, Münsterplatz</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beethoven's return to Vienna from Heiligenstadt was marked by a change in musical style, and is now designated as the start of his middle or "heroic" period. According to Carl Czerny, Beethoven said, "I am not satisfied with the work I have done so far. From now on I intend to take a new way."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008131_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008131-63">[63]</a></sup> This "heroic" phase was characterised by a large number of original works composed on a grand scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The first major work employing this new style was the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Beethoven)">Third Symphony</a> in E flat, known as the <i>Eroica</i>. This work was longer and larger in scope than any previous symphony. When it premiered in early 1805 it received a mixed reception. Some listeners objected to its length or misunderstood its structure, while others viewed it as a masterpiece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008148_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008148-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small-left" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;float:left">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_van_Beethoven_-_Symphonie_5_c-moll_-_1._Allegro_con_brio.ogg" title="File:Ludwig van Beethoven - Symphonie 5 c-moll - 1. Allegro con brio.ogg">Symphony No. 5, Op. 67 (1st movement)</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="436.02222222222" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Ludwig_van_Beethoven_-_Symphonie_5_c-moll_-_1._Allegro_con_brio.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">

</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">composed during Beethoven's middle period</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
<hr/>
<span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The "middle period" is sometimes associated with a "heroic" manner of composing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solomon1990_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon1990-66">[66]</a></sup> but the use of the term "heroic" has become increasingly controversial in Beethoven scholarship. The term is more frequently used as an alternative name for the middle period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steinberg2006_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steinberg2006-67">[67]</a></sup> The appropriateness of the term "heroic" to describe the whole middle period has been questioned as well: while some works, like the Third and Fifth Symphonies, are easy to describe as "heroic", many others, like his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._6_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 6 (Beethoven)">Symphony No. 6, <i>Pastoral</i></a>, are not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurnhamSteinberg2000_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-BurnhamSteinberg2000-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the middle period works extend the musical language Beethoven had inherited from Haydn and Mozart. The middle period work includes the Third through <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._8_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 8 (Beethoven)">Eighth</a> Symphonies, the <a href="/wiki/String_Quartets_Nos._7%E2%80%939,_Op._59_%E2%80%93_Rasumovsky_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartets Nos. 7–9, Op. 59 – Rasumovsky (Beethoven)">Rasumovsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._10_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 10 (Beethoven)"><i>Harp</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._11_(Beethoven)" title="String Quartet No. 11 (Beethoven)"><i>Serioso</i></a> string quartets, the <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._21_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 21 (Beethoven)">Waldstein</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._23_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 23 (Beethoven)">Appassionata</a></i> piano sonatas, <i>Christ on the Mount of Olives</i>, the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Beethoven)" title="Violin Concerto (Beethoven)">Violin Concerto</a> and many other compositions. During this time Beethoven's income came from publishing his works, from performances of them, and from his patrons. His position at the Theater an der Wien was terminated when the theatre changed management in early 1804, and he was forced to move temporarily to the suburbs of Vienna with his friend Stephan von Breuning. This slowed work on <i>Fidelio</i>, his largest work to date, for a time. It was delayed again by the Austrian <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censor</a>, and finally premiered in November 1805 to houses that were nearly empty because of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Third_Coalition" title="War of the Third Coalition">French occupation of the city</a>. In addition to being a financial failure, this version of <i>Fidelio</i> was also a critical failure, and Beethoven began revising it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008150_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008150-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>During May 1809, when the attacking forces of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wagram#Initial_hostilities" title="Battle of Wagram">bombarded Vienna</a>, according to Ferdinand Ries, Beethoven, very worried that the noise would destroy what remained of his hearing, hid in the basement of his brother's house, covering his ears with pillows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008185_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008185-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The work of the middle period established Beethoven as a master. In a review from 1810, he was enshrined by <a href="/wiki/E._T._A._Hoffmann" title="E. T. A. Hoffmann">E. T. A. Hoffmann</a> as one of the three great "<a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic</a>" composers; Hoffman called Beethoven's <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">Fifth Symphony</a> "one of the most important works of the age."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_and_family_difficulties">Personal and family difficulties</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven's love life was hampered by class issues. In late 1801 he met a young countess, <a href="/wiki/Giulietta_Guicciardi" title="Giulietta Guicciardi">Julie ("Giulietta") Guicciardi</a> through the Brunsvik family, at a time when he was giving regular piano lessons to <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Brunsvik" title="Josephine Brunsvik">Josephine Brunsvik</a>. Beethoven mentions his love for Julie in a November 1801 letter to his boyhood friend, Franz Wegeler, but he could not consider marrying her, due to the class difference. Beethoven later dedicated to her his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._14_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 14 (Beethoven)">Sonata No. 14</a>, now commonly known as the <i>Moonlight sonata</i> or <i>Mondscheinsonate</i> (in German).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteblin2009_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteblin2009-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:FurElise.ogg" title="File:FurElise.ogg">Für Elise</a></div>
<div>

</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
<hr/>
<span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>His relationship with Josephine Brunsvik deepened after the death in 1804 of her aristocratic first husband, the Count Joseph Deym. Beethoven wrote Josephine 15 passionate love letters from late 1804 to around 1809/10. Although his feelings were obviously reciprocated, Josephine was forced by her family to withdraw from him in 1807. She cited her "duty" and the fact that she would have lost the custodianship of her aristocratic children had she married a commoner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008146.2C_168_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008146.2C_168-72">[72]</a></sup> After Josephine married Baron von Stackelberg in 1810, Beethoven may have proposed unsuccessfully to <a href="/wiki/Therese_Malfatti" title="Therese Malfatti">Therese Malfatti</a>, the supposed dedicatee of "<a href="/wiki/F%C3%BCr_Elise" title="Für Elise">Für Elise</a>";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorenz2011_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorenz2011-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteblin2014_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteblin2014-74">[74]</a></sup> his status as a commoner may again have interfered with those plans.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1190" data-file-width="893" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg/170px-Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg/255px-Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg/340px-Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masque_de_Beethoven.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_mask" title="Life mask">Life mask</a> made in 1812</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the spring of 1811 Beethoven became seriously ill, suffering headaches and high fever. On the advice of his doctor, he spent six weeks in the <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemian</a> <a href="/wiki/Spa_town" title="Spa town">spa town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Teplice" title="Teplice">Teplitz</a>. The following winter, which was dominated by work on the Seventh symphony, he was again ill, and his doctor ordered him to spend the summer of 1812 at the spa Teplitz. It is certain that he was at Teplitz when he wrote a love letter to his "<a href="/wiki/Immortal_Beloved" title="Immortal Beloved">Immortal Beloved</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandenburg1996582_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandenburg1996582-75">[75]</a></sup> The identity of the intended recipient has long been a subject of debate; candidates include Julie Guicciardi, Therese Malfatti, Josephine Brunsvik, and Antonie Brentano.</p>
<p>Beethoven visited his brother Johann at the end of October 1812. He wished to end Johann's cohabitation with Therese Obermayer, a woman who already had an illegitimate child. He was unable to convince Johann to end the relationship and appealed to the local civic and religious authorities. Johann and Therese married on 9 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008212_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008212-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Letronne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="493" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beethoven_Letronne.jpg/170px-Beethoven_Letronne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beethoven_Letronne.jpg/255px-Beethoven_Letronne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beethoven_Letronne.jpg/340px-Beethoven_Letronne.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_Letronne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Beethoven in 1814. Portrait by Louis-René Létronne.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In early 1813 Beethoven apparently went through a difficult emotional period, and his compositional output dropped. His personal appearance degraded—it had generally been neat—as did his manners in public, especially when dining. Beethoven took care of his brother (who was suffering from tuberculosis) and his family, an expense that he claimed left him penniless.</p>
<p>Beethoven was finally motivated to begin significant composition again in June 1813, when news arrived of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vitoria" title="Battle of Vitoria">defeat of one of Napoleon's armies</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitoria,_Spain" title="Vitoria, Spain">Vitoria, Spain</a>, by a coalition of forces under the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>. This news stimulated him to write the battle symphony known as <i><a href="/wiki/Wellington%27s_Victory" title="Wellington's Victory">Wellington's Victory</a></i>. It was first performed on 8 December, along with his Seventh Symphony, at a charity concert for victims of the war. The work was a popular hit, probably because of its programmatic style, which was entertaining and easy to understand. It received repeat performances at concerts Beethoven staged in January and February 1814. Beethoven's renewed popularity led to demands for a revival of <i>Fidelio</i>, which, in its third revised version, was also well received at its July opening. That summer he composed a piano sonata for the first time in five years (<i><a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._27_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 27 (Beethoven)">No. 27, Opus 90</a></i>). This work was in a markedly more <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> style than his earlier sonatas. He was also one of many composers who produced music in a patriotic vein to entertain the many heads of state and diplomats who came to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> that began in November 1814. His output of songs included his only <a href="/wiki/Song_cycle" title="Song cycle">song cycle</a>, "<a href="/wiki/An_die_ferne_Geliebte" title="An die ferne Geliebte">An die ferne Geliebte</a>," and the extraordinarily expressive second setting of the poem "An die Hoffnung" (Op. 94) in 1815. Compared to its first setting in 1805 (a gift for Josephine Brunsvik), it was "far more dramatic ... The entire spirit is that of an operatic scena."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood2005278_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood2005278-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Custody_struggle_and_illness">Custody struggle and illness</span></h3>
<p>Between 1815 and 1817 Beethoven's output dropped again. Beethoven attributed part of this to a lengthy illness (he called it an "inflammatory fever") that afflicted him for more than a year, starting in October 1816.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008254_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008254-78">[78]</a></sup> Biographers have speculated on a variety of other reasons that also contributed to the decline, including the difficulties in the personal lives of his would-be paramours and the harsh censorship policies of the Austrian government. The illness and death of his brother Kaspar from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> may also have played a role.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="440" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/170px-Beethoven_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/255px-Beethoven_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Beethoven_7.jpg/340px-Beethoven_7.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beethoven_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Beethoven in 1818 by August Klöber</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Kaspar had been ill for some time, and Beethoven spent a small fortune in 1815 on his care. After Kaspar died on 15 November 1815, Beethoven immediately became embroiled in a protracted legal dispute with Kaspar's wife <a href="/wiki/Johanna_van_Beethoven" title="Johanna van Beethoven">Johanna</a> over custody of their son <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Karl_van_Beethoven_(born_1806)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Karl van Beethoven (born 1806) (page does not exist)">Karl</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_van_Beethoven" title="de:Karl van Beethoven">de</a>)</span>, then nine years old. Beethoven, who considered Johanna an unfit parent because of her morals (she had an illegitimate child by a different father before marrying Kaspar and had been convicted of theft) and financial management, had successfully applied to Kaspar to have himself named sole guardian of the boy. A late <a href="/wiki/Codicil_(will)" title="Codicil (will)">codicil</a> to Kaspar's will gave him and Johanna joint guardianship. While Beethoven was successful at having his nephew removed from her custody in February 1816, the case was not fully resolved until 1820, and he was frequently preoccupied by the demands of the litigation and seeing to the welfare of Karl, whom he first placed in a private school.</p>
<p>The Austrian court system had one court for the <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> and members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landtafel" title="Landtafel">Landtafel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Landrecht_(medieval)" title="Landrecht (medieval)">Landrechte</a>, and many other courts for commoners, among them the Civil Court of the Vienna Magistrate. Beethoven disguised the fact that the Dutch "<a href="/wiki/Van_(Dutch)" title="Van (Dutch)">van</a>" in his name did not denote nobility as does the German "<a href="/wiki/Von" title="Von">von</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landrecht_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-landrecht-79">[79]</a></sup> and his case was tried in the Landrechte. Owing to his influence with the court, Beethoven felt assured of the favourable outcome of being awarded sole guardianship. While giving evidence to the Landrechte, however, Beethoven was unable to prove being of noble birth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landrecht_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-landrecht-79">[79]</a></sup> and as a consequence, on 18 December 1818 the case was transferred to the Magistracy, where he lost sole guardianship.</p>
<p>Beethoven appealed and regained custody. Johanna's appeal to the Emperor was not successful: the Emperor "washed his hands of the matter." During the years of custody that followed, Beethoven attempted to ensure that Karl lived to the highest moral standards. Beethoven had an overbearing manner and frequently interfered in his nephew's life. Karl attempted suicide on 31 July 1826 by shooting himself in the head. He survived and was brought to his mother's house, where he recuperated. He and Beethoven were reconciled, but Karl insisted on joining the army and last saw Beethoven in January 1827.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_works">Late works</span></h3>
<p>Beethoven began a renewed study of older music, including works by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">J. S. Bach</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a>, that were then being published in the first attempts at complete editions. He composed the overture <i><a href="/wiki/The_Consecration_of_the_House_(overture)" title="The Consecration of the House (overture)">The Consecration of the House</a></i>, which was the first work to attempt to incorporate these influences. A new style emerged, now called his "late period". He returned to the keyboard to compose his first piano sonatas in almost a decade: the works of the late period are commonly held to include the last five piano sonatas and the <i><a href="/wiki/Diabelli_Variations" title="Diabelli Variations">Diabelli Variations</a></i>, the last two sonatas for cello and piano, the late string quartets (see below), and two works for very large forces: the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missa_Solemnis_(Beethoven)" title="Missa Solemnis (Beethoven)">Missa Solemnis</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>By early 1818 Beethoven's health had improved, and his nephew moved in with him in January. On the downside, his hearing had deteriorated to the point that conversation became difficult, necessitating the use of conversation books. His household management had also improved somewhat; Nanette Streicher, who had assisted in his care during his illness, continued to provide some support, and he finally found a skilled cook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008260_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008260-81">[81]</a></sup> His musical output in 1818 was still somewhat reduced, but included song collections and the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._29_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Sonata No. 29 (Beethoven)">"Hammerklavier" Sonata</a>, as well as sketches for two symphonies that eventually coalesced into the epic Ninth. In 1819 he was again preoccupied by the legal processes around Karl, and began work on the <i><a href="/wiki/Diabelli_Variations" title="Diabelli Variations">Diabelli Variations</a></i> and the <i>Missa Solemnis</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>For the next few years he continued to work on the Missa, composing piano sonatas and <a href="/wiki/Bagatelle_(music)" title="Bagatelle (music)">bagatelles</a> to satisfy the demands of publishers and the need for income, and completing the Diabelli Variations. He was ill again for an extended time in 1821, and completed the Missa in 1823, three years after its original due date. He also opened discussions with his publishers over the possibility of producing a complete edition of his work, an idea that was arguably not fully realised until 1971.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Beethoven's brother Johann began to take a hand in his business affairs, much in the way Kaspar had earlier, locating older unpublished works to offer for publication and offering the Missa to multiple publishers with the goal of getting a higher price for it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Two commissions in 1822 improved Beethoven's financial prospects. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philharmonic_Society" title="Philharmonic Society">Philharmonic Society</a> of London offered a commission for a symphony, and Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolay_Borisovich_Galitzin" title="Nikolay Borisovich Galitzin">Nikolas Golitsin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a> offered to pay Beethoven's price for three string quartets. The first of these commissions spurred Beethoven to finish the Ninth Symphony, which was first performed, along with the Missa Solemnis, on 7 May 1824, to great acclaim at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C3%A4rntnertortheater" title="Kärntnertortheater">Kärntnertortheater</a>. The <i>Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung</i> gushed, "inexhaustible genius had shown us a new world," and Carl Czerny wrote that his symphony "breathes such a fresh, lively, indeed youthful spirit ... so much power, innovation, and beauty as ever [came] from the head of this original man, although he certainly sometimes led the old wigs to shake their heads." Unlike his more lucrative earlier concerts, this did not make Beethoven much money, as the expenses of mounting it were significantly higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008317_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008317-82">[82]</a></sup> A second concert on 24 May, in which the producer guaranteed Beethoven a minimum fee, was poorly attended; nephew Karl noted that "many people [had] already gone into the country." It was Beethoven's last public concert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008318_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008318-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">

<div class="center">

</div>
</td>
